                            <div class="player-info-box-header "><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>Danny Espinosa</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team">New York Yankees (AAA)</div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 30</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: B/R</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-item">6' 0" / 205</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-pos">2B/SS</div></div>
                        <div class="player-info-bio">
                            <strong>Birthdate: </strong>4/25/1987 (30 y, 10 m, 13 d)<br /><b>Drafted:</b> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=9219&position=2B/SS#draft" style="text-decoration:none;">2008 June Amateur Draft - Round: 3, Pick: 10, Overall: 87, Team: Washington Nationals</a><br /><strong>Contract:</strong> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=9219&position=2B/SS#contract" >$5.4M / 1 Years (2017)</a>

            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>The Nationals like power, and Danny Espinosa has more than you generally see in a middle infielder, which is why he’s being given a chance to win the second base job despite some flaws in the other parts of his game – notably, his pension for swinging and missing.  Always a guy who whiffed in the minors, Espinosa carried that over to the big leagues, striking out in 29.1% of his at-bats and hitting just .214 in a trial run last year.  However, his defense continued to impress and half of his hits went for extra bases, so there are tools there that could allow Espinosa to succeed in the big leagues.  I might be more optimistic about 2012, however – he had surgery on his hamate bone over the winter, and that’s an injury that is notorious for draining a hitter’s power.  If he’s not driving the ball, the strikeouts could be tough to handle, and Espinosa might find himself back in Triple-A to get more seasoning.  Draft cautiously.   (Dave Cameron)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Enough talent to win the starting job, but questionable approach at the plate and the possibility of a lingering injury provide bundles of risk.  Don't target him if you need a guy who will definitely stay in the big leagues.  </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>It's hard to strikeout 15 percentage points more often than you walk and still manage to post a 3.5 WAR or better. Here, for example, is a complete list of the players who did it in 2011: Mike Stanton (11.6% BB, 27.6% K, 4.5 WAR), Peter Bourjos (5.8%, 22.5%, 4.3), and Danny Espinosa (8.7%, 25.2%, 3.5). Stanton and Bourjos are both players basically without peer -- Stanton for the sort of power he possesses at a young age; Bourjos, for his combination of raw speed and fielding instincts. Espinosa is singular in his own way, combining power, speed, and defensive acumen in the middle of the infield. While that final trait doesn't contribute directly to fantasy value, it does mean that he could slide over to shortstop if (when?) the Nats decide that incumbent Ian Desmond is ill suited for the starting role.  (Carson Cistulli)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Espinosa has struck out in a little over 25% of his major-league plate appearances, and will likely continue to approximate that rate in 2011; however, his power and speed -- combined with possible shortstop eligibility -- make him interesting.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Strikeouts are becoming a pretty significant problem for Danny Espinosa. The NL leader in the category, Espinosa managed to strikeout in 28.7% of his plate appearances last year. His .247 average was actually an improvement over his rookie season, but he'll never be a candidate to post a strong average if the can't corral the whiffs. He does make the most of his other skills, hitting a fair amount of home runs for a second baseman and stealing some bases. He's probably not a candidate to see a massive improvement, but he can have value if you can deal with his poor average -- perhaps by punting the category in a head-to-head league. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Chris_Cwik">Chris Cwik</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Espinosa will never post a strong batting average, but does enough to have some value in fantasy leagues. He'll provide a nice skill set as a late round middle infielder, but you wouldn't feel great going into the year with him as your starter.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>There are many ways to fail as a major league player. One such way is to post a 0.09 walk to strikeout ratio. Espinosa struck out 47 times against just four walks. He also forgot how to hit line drives, which led to a cratered batting average on balls in play and a .158/.193/.272 line over 167 plate appearances. A trip to Triple-A led to better results but not by much. Espinosa will likely get a chance to refocus and rebuild his value as a backup to Ian Desmond and Anthony Rendon. If he makes good on his opportunities, he may find a full-time role with another team. He's just a year removed from a 17 home run, 20 stolen base season. While he'll kill fantasy owners in the batting average category, that combination of power and speed is hard to find in the middle infield. (Brad Johnson)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Espinosa crumbled in 2013. He entered the season as a flawed upside play with plenty of power and speed, but the Nationals kicked him to the curb in early June. He's young and athletic, but there's no need to draft him.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>There’s a chance Espinosa will open the year as the Nationals’ second baseman, and that at least makes him intriguing. In the past, he’s shown the ability to post strong power and steals totals, but that comes at the expense of his batting average. On top of that, Espinosa’s on-base percentage is dependent on him getting hit by a fair amount of pitches. Espinosa has always had issues batting from the left side, and the club may look to end his switch-hitting experiment soon. Manager Matt Williams announced that Espinosa will attempt to hit from the right side against some right-handers during spring training. It’s an interesting idea, and could boost Espinosa’s value slightly. Expectations should remain small, but there’s at least some use here if Espinosa gets the full-time job. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Chris_Cwik">Chris Cwik</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Espinosa has shown a strong power and speed combination in the past, and that should make him an interesting fantasy option. He hasn’t hit for average in the past, and Yunel Escobar is probably the favorite for the starting job, and that limits Espinosa's upside.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>The 28-year-old Espinosa's fantasy value has taken a roller coaster ride after his first full season in 2011 when he hit 21 homers with 17 stolen bases. Just a couple of years later, he hit only .158 with three homers. In 2015, he seemed to put a few things together and hit 13 HR and raised his average to .240. Well, the increase was not enough for the Nationals. Even with Ian Desmond leaving the team to open a hole at shortstop, the Nats brought in Daniel Murphy to play second and an internal promotion, Trea Turner, might yet play short. Additionally, the Nationals signed Stephen Drew who can play the in middle infield. Right now Espinosa may have been regulated to utility infielder with the best chance of playing time coming from a trade. Even if he is able to secure some playing time over Turner, his value still isn’t going to be high. Espinosa can hit the ball hard and will continue to get his home runs. With 600 plate appearances, 20 homers would still be a possibility. His stolen bases are way down, but he has been successful the past two seasons, going 13 for 16, so stealing ten this year is a distinct possibility. The biggest drag on his fantasy value are his strikeouts (28% career strikeout rate) and the resulting low average (.230 career AVG). His best case scenario could be 20 HR, 10 SB and a .250 AVG. The low side could be a huge pile of stinking dog poo. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Danny Espinosa has been a fringe hitter for a few years now and the Nationals found some replacements for him. He could be starting for the team at shortstop now, but there's also a hot young prospect -- Trea Turner -- pushing up behind him.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>With Ian Desmond out of the picture, Danny Espinosa received more playing time last year than he had since 2012, knocking in a career-high 24 home runs in 157 games. Unfortunately, the rest of Espinosa's offensive shortcomings stuck around -- Espi's average fell to .209, he continued to strike out in about 30% of his plate appearances, and his 9 stolen bases were a far cry from the 20 he swiped back in 2012. With the emergence of Trea Turner, though, and the Nationals' trade for Adam Eaton, Espinosa was set to ride the bench once again -- until he was sent to the Angels in December. Espinosa looks likely to slot in as the Angels' 2B, where he should continue to hit a respectable number of home runs and strike out a lot, possibly with slightly lower R/RBI totals in the Angels' weaker lineup. He might have some value as a backup MI, but have a plan to offset his Mendoza-esque average if you start him. (Brice Russ)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Espinosa finally played a full season last year, but got shipped to Anaheim after his starting role with the Nats vanished. He's not without value, but his solid pop doesn't make up for what will likely be a distressingly low batting average.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>
